Spring and Autumn

Righteousness and Profit 235

— Yan Qiao

The day before yesterday, Mr. Tang Weizhi gave a lecture on Mencius at the Suzhou National Studies Society, saying that the seven chapters of Mencius were simply about the distinction between righteousness and profit. He also lamented that in recent times, there is a situation of "both superiors and inferiors contending for profit." He spoke very thoroughly, not like the commentaries on the Thirteen Classics, explaining the classics with the classics, leaving people confused and ultimately at a loss.

In my opinion, today's China is much better than the China Mencius saw. And those kings, those officials, those scholars and common people, are also much more admirable. Just look at the speeches published in the newspapers, which one of them isn't thinking about plans for the welfare of the country and the people? They don't talk about benefiting the country or the family, but about benefiting the people. The word "country" is too abstract, while the word "people" is so practical. As for the talk of competing with the people for profit, it only circulates in their stomachs, and they never dare to let it slip out of their mouths. This is also the result of more than two thousand years of traditional cultivation.

Although the rights and obligations are listed equally in the provisional constitution, we only hear people shouting about "outflow of rights and interests," and wanting to "recover rights and interests." They are afraid that people will not compete for profit. Looking at it from another perspective, there used to be righteous servants, righteous maids, righteous thieves, and righteous beggars in the notes, and even dogs of different races also had righteousness. Could it be that they have all been eliminated along with the eight-legged essays and queues? Or perhaps they are unwilling to see the development of such distorted private virtues again? Therefore, newspapers that record everything they hear have discarded these things into the wastepaper basket.

I am not advocating for martial arts novels. Indeed, seeing all kinds of injustices in society, and feeling full of strange anger, I can only vent it through them. When we open the newspaper, aside from the masked performers on the world stage and the disorganized magic show on the national stage, it is all photography of lewdness and theft. Especially in the descriptions of men and women, they never compromise on a single word, and never relax on a single sentence. In their view, is this benefiting themselves and benefiting others?

"The beauty already belongs to the Shali, and there are no righteous warriors like the ancient escorts." Just cross out six words, and it can accurately describe the modern era. It is "The beauty belongs to profit, and there are no righteous warriors." As for the interpretation of beauty, it is certainly not in the narrow sense.

Women 236

— Sleeping Buddha

Originally, since we are "people," we naturally speak human words, eat human food, fulfill human obligations, and enjoy human rights. Everything is done to truly "be a person." Then, as human beings, we do what we do as human beings, not what someone orders us to do. Where is there room for someone to give us those appellations that deviate from "human," shaking our position as "people"? Yet there are clever and selfish people who, just because of that slight difference in physiology, painstakingly come up with a "woman" to put above "people," to distinguish and describe a kind of mystery. At the same time, there are really brainless people who actually accept it. Thus, an invisible shackle is placed on them, and people live and compete in this shackle forever. Just look: in the past, they competed to be virtuous women, chaste women... now they have transformed into social butterflies, queens... there are also those who are half-awakened, rushing to pursue women's rights, women's movements... How pitiful! They—in fact, they are them—are the ones in shackles. Thinking that since they are "women," they naturally only do what women do, and don't ask about anything else. In other words, they only know that they are "women," and never know that they are "people."

Is this the crime of women? The fact is indeed displayed before our eyes. In the midst of national crisis: the clothes on their bodies, the powder on their faces, and the dazzling decorations, the enchanting perfumes, which one of them is not an imported product, a high-priced imported product? They spend their days dancing, watching plays, eating fancy food, riding in cars, especially sensual. They take away people's money, consume people's energy. They... Such extravagance and lewdness undoubtedly make them the object of ridicule, the target of blame, and the source of sighs for gentlemen. Moreover, in the face of national crisis, one cannot help but think that the occurrence of the national crisis, the inability to save the national crisis, is mostly due to their extravagance and lewdness. However, however, is this really their crime? The society of private property has long treated them as private property, as commodities, suppressing them, intimidating them, and tempting them, causing them to lose their instinctive resistance and become obedient. At the same time, they are made into toys for the upper classes. Is this also their crime? Alas! The social system squeezes them into all kinds of slaves. How pitiful! Being squeezed into slaves, and still having the crime added to their heads, is even more pitiful!

— Yan Chonglou

Ibsen said, "The most rational person in the world is the most isolated person." From this sentence, we can know the importance of power to personal relationships! An athlete who can maintain a record that others cannot break, a great writer who can be admired by readers all over the world, is this not clear evidence of the supremacy of power?

If you doubt power because "a weak person can kill a strong person with a pistol. A mentally exhausted person can suddenly feel energetic after smoking two puffs of opium," then you must pay attention to the fact that the weak person and the strong person both don't have a pistol, and that smoking opium is self-destructive.

Friends, develop and train your own power! Develop and train your correct power!

Expectation 238

Recently, I have often been troubled by various kinds of expectations: such as expecting rain in my hometown, but my hometown stubbornly refuses to rain; expecting friends to keep their promises, but friends stubbornly refuse to keep their promises, and so on.

Life is originally in the midst of expectations, but the value of expectations lies in being able to fulfill them. If it is always expectations, then it is no different from dreams! Comparing the two, perhaps dreams are even better, because dreams are short-lived, while expectations take a lot of time, and the result is only disappointment and disillusionment.

Seeing that the sky is not raining, it is better to prepare for artificial irrigation methods as soon as possible. As for friends who do not keep their promises, it is better not to invite them at all. This will save a lot of trouble!

Empty expectations are of no help to reality.

Misunderstanding 239

Thorough understanding is necessary, but misunderstandings often occur between people.

"Misunderstanding and laziness are probably more harmful than fraud and evil." Goethe once said this. He also said: "To be misunderstood is originally the fate of us human beings"! Yes, misunderstanding is unfortunate. In our lives, we often misunderstand others, and we are often misunderstood by others. From these misunderstandings, various mistakes are sometimes inevitably made. In fact, there are many, many things in the world that are misunderstood, such as an ideology that is considered progressive by A, but considered reactionary by B!

Everyone has their own position, and misunderstanding is due to incompleteness. If you don't want to misunderstand, you must thoroughly explore. If you can't thoroughly explore, then misunderstanding is inevitable. This is my conclusion about misunderstanding.

Mao Dun and "Spring Silkworms" 240

— Jingru

Undoubtedly, Mao Dun is the most diligent one in the current literary world.

He not only produces more than other writers,

but also has more substance in his works than other writers. We rarely see a careless novel from him, even a chapter, a section, and a sentence are always carefully planned, arranged, and structured.

In the past two years, Mr. Mao Dun has abandoned the recollections of the May Fourth era and focused on exposing and guiding the society that is currently in darkness. This is clearly reflected in his works during this period. "Spring Silkworms" can be regarded as a representative work of this kind.

In "Spring Silkworms," Mr. Mao Dun's realistic skills are unexpected. The story takes place in a village in central Jiangsu or near Zhejiang. He not only writes about the local customs and human feelings in this area so that people feel as if they are there, but also describes the individual personalities of two generations of people in the countryside very realistically. In addition, the suffering that the current society inflicts on the villagers is also cried out between the lines without any exaggeration. This is something that other new literary writers who use rural reality as their subject matter cannot match.

Now, this story has been adopted by the Star Film Company and adapted and produced into a movie. And the director is Mr. Cheng Bugao, who is unique in his realistic skills. I think "Spring Silkworms" will inevitably become the most successful realistic film today.

The new literary world and the film world have successfully joined hands. We look forward to the emergence of new literary works on the screen following "Spring Silkworms," and a closer integration of literature and film!

Short Stories

The Madman (Part 1) 242

— Mo Yi

One week ago, one night, the weather was so hot and stuffy that I simply couldn't close my eyes unless I forced myself to sleep with sleeping pills. Later, I really couldn't stand it, so I quietly got out of bed, turned on the light, washed my face with cold water, wiped my body, and pulled out a copy of the "Novel Monthly" from a pile of old books and lay on the rattan chair, flipping through it aimlessly.

Reading under the incandescent light seemed even hotter. Sweat was streaming down non-stop, and the clothes on my body were as wet as if I had just climbed out of a swimming pool. I had to move the rattan chair to the window,

turned off the light, lay on it, constantly waving a fan, and stared out the window with wide eyes.

The night was quiet, and there was no sound. The moon was hanging in the sky, already missing a corner. There were no clouds. Stars were sparsely scattered on the blue sky. Occasionally, a gust of wind came, but it was hot; blowing on the body, it might blow out a burst of sweat.

What a beautiful moonlit night this is. It's a pity it's hot!

I was thinking wildly. My thoughts were like a horse that had broken free of its reins, not knowing where it had wandered off to. What I was actually thinking about, I don't know. I only know that I was thinking wildly. The moon, the stars, the clouds, the wind, the sun, the rain, the snow, the tragic changes in my family, the education expenses for my two children in the future, my wife's frequent illnesses, the Japanese occupation of the four northeastern provinces, the fight between my black cat and the yellow cat that lived with me, it seemed that I thought about everything, it seemed that everything had passed through my mind.

But, I was still hot, and sweat was still streaming down. I also felt more irritable.

The clock next door struck twice.

"Aooo... Aooo..." It sounded neither like a sheep bleating nor like a wolf howling. "Ah, a ghost is howling"!

I jumped straight up from the rattan chair. The hairs on my body all stood on end. A cold sweat drove away the stuffiness.

"Aooo... Aooo... Slut, do you want to see? I'm not wearing pants! Haha, haha! Aooo... Aooo..."

I realized that this was a person speaking, so I breathed a sigh of relief. My whole body also "lifted martial law." I gradually started to feel hot again.

But, why did he have to "Aooo..."?

"Aooo... Little brother-in-law, haha, little brother-in-law, little turtle, I admit defeat in front of you! Let's settle the score in the next life. Evil will be punished, little turtle, listen, don't be so ruthless! Aooo..."

The sound was coming from across the street. I stood at the window and looked across the street. But I didn't see a single person. This guy was "Aooo..."ing more and more vigorously, and the sound was becoming more and more miserable. I couldn't bear to listen. But, I was still driven by curiosity, so I still stood there looking across the street, quietly listening to this miserable sound.

I had lived here for more than three months, and I had never heard such a sound before. Maybe this guy often "Aooo..."s randomly, but I can't say for sure; however, I haven't had the chance to hear it. Borrowing the moonlight, I glanced at my wife on the bed and felt that women at home had nothing to do, and loved to gossip about other people's business, and that my wife must know about this matter. I thought about waking up my wife and asking her who was shouting like this in the middle of the night, and why no one was interfering. I walked to the bed and, looking at my wife's smiling face in her deep sleep, I didn't want to wake her up after her recent illness. I planned to ask her tomorrow. So I went back to the window, lay on the rattan chair, and listened to this sound that aroused my inexplicable sadness. I kept listening until the sky had already turned fish-belly white, and then I vaguely fell asleep amidst the miserable sound.

When I woke up the next morning, it was already 8:45. I hurriedly wiped my face and went to the company to work. I forgot to ask my wife about what happened last night. When I got to the company, I was ordered to go to a branch company in another city to investigate a matter, and when I came back three days later, I just forgot about it. Today, after eating dinner, I was pulled to the entrance by Beibei (my five-year-old daughter) to cool off. There were many people in the alley. I was listening to them talking about gambling with great interest when suddenly the back door of the house across the street opened, and a man jumped out from inside. He was wearing a rimless straw hat on his head, a small vest on his upper body, and dragging a pair of broken shoes on his bare feet, but, ah, he was not wearing pants!

Free Talk

Literacy and Word Usage 268

— Gao Zhi

A few days ago, I saw Zhecun Jun asking people to read Zhuangzi and Wenxuan, and I wanted to say a few words. Yesterday, I saw Feng Zhiyu Jun's article, and besides feeling that Shi Jun was indeed a bit "oversensitive," I also had some doubts about his words.

High school students' "vocabulary" is too small, we quite acknowledge this. However, Shi Jun saw high school students' articles and had this experience. I have also seen high school students' articles (this has nothing to do with my resume), and my opinion is not as general as Shi Jun's. There are two aspects to looking at the small vocabulary of high school students (I have this, and it is not limited to high school students): one is that they use few words, and the other is that they read few words. Usually, the words we use are certainly fewer than the words we know. Shi Jun likes to use old vocabulary, but we dare not say that Shi Jun's vocabulary is only the words he uses. High school students' small vocabulary is not necessarily because they know few words, but because their age and experience are limited, and they cannot yet use the words they know. I myself have had this experience when writing English compositions. Knowing many words does not necessarily mean that you will use them. This is my opinion of Shi Jun.

Even if high school students' small vocabulary is due to knowing few words, it is quite questionable whether Wenxuan and Zhuangzi can increase their vocabulary. It is also questionable how many of the words in these two books are currently usable. We sometimes feel that Shi Jun's occasional use of words from mountain and sea fu in his novels is difficult. We recognize more vocabulary sometimes for reading ancient books, and sometimes for knowledge, and we do not necessarily have to show it in articles. Although word usage and literacy are "related," they are not the same thing.

Can Zhuangzi and Wenxuan really enable people to understand the method of writing articles? This may be true for some people. But I am doubtful. Mr. Yu Dafu said that Chinese new literature is inherited from the world literature system. Even Shi Jun's novels and poems may be using foreign methods, right? Or perhaps Shi Jun is "oversensitive" and has unique insights, but one person's opinion may not enable others to obtain the same. I believe that current articles are gradually adopting foreign methods more and more. Naturally, our ancestors have also left us quite a bit. I feel that asking high school students to read Wenxuan and Zhuangzi to learn article writing methods and increase their vocabulary is an effort that yields little result. If someone actually believes Shi Jun's words and reads Wenxuan and Zhuangzi, and is going to learn article writing methods and increase their vocabulary, what results will Shi Jun think of once they apply it?

"Without the cultivation of classical literature, Lu Xun Jiesheng's new articles would never have been written as well as they are now." Why can't many scholars of Chinese classical studies write new articles? This is a question of whether thinking and concepts are mature. If thinking and concepts are mature, and there is enough vocabulary, articles can definitely be written. Otherwise, reading ten Zhuangzis is useless. Currently, there are many people who write articles very well, and I know that they have nothing to do with Zhuangzi and Wenxuan. Instead of having high school students spend time reading Zhuangzi and Wenxuan, looking up dictionaries, and learning article writing methods, it is better to use this effort to train their thinking and concepts and increase their new knowledge. But I am not against looking up dictionaries and reading classical literature. Although Shi Jun does not want young people to all do classical Chinese, these two classical Chinese works cannot give young people the results that Shi Jun expects.

Notes on "Letters of Zhou Zuoren" 269

— Huai Qiu

October 6th, Friday. I woke up not too late, before seven o'clock.

I met Xiaolin in the cafeteria and borrowed "Letters of Zhou Zuoren" from him. Reading books for enjoyment—this year marks a great transformation and can be called a great improvement. I started by reading the preface, which I found very appealing, so I took it to the lecture hall – to a lecture by a certain Mr. Yu Xiang – and while Mr. Yu's voice wasn't loud, I focused on the book. Occasionally, I looked up at his eyes, feeling like I exchanged glances with the blackboard once or twice, or only faced Mr. So-and-so from my hometown. Therefore, I didn't feel like I was attracting much attention, making this my first major success reading in the lecture hall. After class, I still couldn't put the book down, even when my stomach was rumbling, until Ming Fei called me to the cafeteria. I closed the book with "letters" on my mind. Pinglin said I was eating too slowly, which was also because I was preoccupied. After the meal, I continued reading, and finished the book in less than half an hour. Then, I rinsed my mouth, closed my eyes, and fell asleep. When I woke up, I felt great. I rinsed my mouth with more hot tea, took a sip or two, and a bee came. I sprayed it with water, and it left, but then came back. So I chased it and sprayed it again. After three or four sprays, the bee lay motionless on the ground, and I went back inside to grind ink and write notes, wanting to discuss Qi Ming's writing. Qi Ming's writing is wonderful. What makes it so wonderful? There are three things: first, I could read it in the lecture hall; second, I didn't notice my stomach rumbling while reading it; and third, I even broke my routine after dinner and read before sleeping. Having one of these qualities would already be quite something, let alone having all three. What is the secret to this wonderfulness? I have an explanation: I say, his writing is fresh in every word and plain in every sentence. This freshness and plainness are very rare these days. Lower-level writers create new things, but there are too many things like "I'm lacking virtue," or "Aki," and even someone with my limited English skills finds it hard to keep up. Of course, I'm not a standard English speaker, and I can't assume that others don't understand just because I don't. Qi Ming's letters certainly have some very new sentences, but they never leave me completely clueless – except for one poem included, which I found quite difficult to understand, but that wasn't written by the old man Qi Ming. Moreover, the recipients he copied were learned scholars like Pingbo, Feiming, and Nan Wuji. I am not as learned, so it's not the copier's fault that I don't understand. The sentences are short, and after being used to reading translations or "originals" with sentences of twenty or thirty words that require a painstaking, word-by-word reading, this feels different. All this is just freshness. Old man Qi Ming doesn't have any doctrines that need to be promoted or any objects that must be praised or overthrown, loved or hated. So, he writes in a plain and simple way, without the so-called risky strokes of a literary master. But I am someone who usually loves to judge people and firmly adhere to right and wrong. So, when I read these letters, I also become calm and feel like looking up at the clouds. This is a great pleasure, and I rarely experience it these days. The old man's life is much better than ours (I boldly use "ours," thinking some of my friends feel the same way). However, when it comes to writing, either there is time but no leisure, or there is leisure but no intention. Writing depends on being "forced" or "repaying debts." Or they keep the "kitten" (this is a sketch that Mr. Qi Ming was planning to write) in their minds for years, letting her meow by the eaves without ever putting her on paper. Then it is excusable that we, this group of poor students who exhaust our minds thinking about life (Note: also about livelihood issues) in the lecture hall, have nothing to write. That explains it completely.

Autumn in the Ancient City 270

— Liao Bo Tan

In the early autumn dusk, an ancient city is shrouded in a deathly silence.

I wander on a weed-covered square. As the wind passes, hinting at the coming winter, there come the hoarse cries of those struggling for life, the wild barking of dogs, and the rustling sounds of withered leaves. The breeze, blowing on my body, is a little chilly. "Ah! It's autumn again!" I think.

In the distance, a group of soldiers sit on the grass resting. It's just "my feeling," but we don't laugh, we don't talk, and our faces seem covered by a layer of gloomy mesh.

An old beggar, emitting a mournful sound, tremblingly, weakly, uses all the pitiful adjectives to describe himself, bestowing every noble crown on others, pleading for the charity of every "good person," but the "good people" all hurry past.

I stare blankly at the leaves of a willow tree: it silently sways in the autumn wind.

All of this tightens my heart, tightly, and I walk forward painfully and lazily, like a crow separated from its flock at dusk.

Looking up, over there the electric lights emit a ghastly white light; the wind is stronger, and the passersby are gradually dwindling: : :

Communication from Ganpu (6) 271

— A Ying

Dear Friend [Names are obscured]:

What kind of structure is the so-called "salt pond"? In my last letter, I already said that on the vast sandy beach, there are mounds of earth that look like graves. These mounds are the sand that has been filtered for salt and repeatedly dug up from the salt pond as "ash waste." Next to them is the "salt pond." The structure of a salt pond is a rectangular depression dug into the ground, with sides built up with mud. The size of the depression varies, but at the very least, it is about ten feet long and seven or eight feet wide. In the middle of the depth of the salt pond, a row of reeds is densely packed, allowing water to barely flow down. At the same time, on the side, there is a deep wooden bucket embedded in the ground to hold the brine. The size and depth of this bucket vary. The ones I saw were two feet deep and one foot in circumference, with round or square covers of one and a half feet. The bucket mouth is connected to the brine pond, allowing the brine to flow out of the pond and into the bucket.

After the ash is poured into the pond, onto the reeds, the next task is "water carrying." Strong male and female workers carry buckets from the salt pond all the way to the seashore, carrying seawater bucket by bucket and pouring it onto the mud in the pond. This allows the water to carry the salt from the mud down through the reeds and into the large wooden bucket for brine, which becomes "salt brine." The filtered soil is then carried to the side of the pond and piled up. When the work is finished, it is spread back onto the ground, allowing the seawater to spread the salt seeds again. This means waiting for the tide to come. Once the brine condenses, it flows into the bucket, which is called a "salt well." However, the process isn't complete yet. The next step is to measure the thickness and concentration of the brine to see its quality. The measuring tool is called "lotus seed." This "lotus seed" is said to be produced in Ningbo, but it is not the edible kind. They use a small oil ladle, place several lotus seeds in the ladle, and cover the mouth of the ladle with wire mesh. They use a bucket to scoop up brine and place the lotus seeds into it. The degree to which the lotus seeds float indicates the quality of the brine. The bucket for holding the brine is sometimes a porcelain jar; however, next to the salt stove, it is mostly made of wood. If the brine is successful, it can be carried to the salt stove at any time to be burned into salt. If not, the bucket mouth can be sealed with "grass tendons."

Here, it is necessary to explain the construction of the "salt stove." A typical stove is very large, usually occupying the space of three rooms. The so-called pot is usually made of six or eight large flat iron plates pieced together. The gaps are constantly filled with mud tendons to prevent leakage. These plates are hung from iron pillars suspended from the roof pillars to prevent them from falling. Below, the stove is made of earth. The stove is about two feet long and one foot wide. Behind the stove are large brine vats and seawater vats, with small brine buckets above them, close to a bucket that holds a salt pan, directly connected to the pan by a small bamboo tube. When burning, first pour the brine into the brine bucket, allowing it to flow out of the bamboo tube, through a basket at the mouth of the tube (used for purifying), and into the pan. This is how salt is made. It takes about one and a half hours to burn a batch of salt. In between, there are also steps, such as stirring in seawater when it gets too dry, and turning it over with a shovel and spreading it evenly when it is about to dry completely, until it becomes salt. After the salt is burned, or rather, before it is fully burned, it can be taken out. This is called "salt essence," but this is only kept by the salt-making family for themselves to eat or use for rinsing their mouths. Generally, it needs to be fully burned, then shoveled into a basket, carried to the warehouse, and stored, then packed and transported out.

That's how salt is made. Here, I would like to introduce some of the names and terms related to salt-making tools. The scraping knife used for scraping ash is also called a "towing bed." Spreading the scraped ash flat is done with a "ash spreader," and the term for spreading it flat is "dropping ash." Digging out old mud is done with an "iron rake," and it's called "digging up the old." Putting in new ash is called "collecting ash," using a "push rake," "rake head," and "silk carrying pole." Then, there is the work of carrying ash to "fill the trough," using "brine buckets" and "dippers." Below, the work of leveling is called "filling the trough," which is the work of pouring water, using "brine buckets." Two days later, the brine is passed into the well, called "jumping the brine" or "settling the brine." As for the work used when burning salt, it is mostly "Y-shaped shovel," "long fire fork," "salt shovel," "scooping brine" material, "stirring the brine dipper," "material bucket," "adjusting ash shovel," "spreading ash rake," etc. As far as I know, it's mostly like this. I wonder if there are any omissions or errors? August 26th

Two Brothers (2) 272

— By Guy de Maupassant, translated by Li Weike

The old fisherman inhaled the stench with gusto, as if it were the fragrance of roses, and said:

"Ah! So fresh, these fish!"

Then he continued:

"How many did you catch! You, doctor?"

His eldest son, Pierre, a thirty-year-old man with a black mustache trimmed like a magistrate's, and clean-shaven lips and chin, replied:

"Ah! Not many, only three or four."

The father turned to ask the younger son:

"And you, Jean?"

Jean, a slender young man with golden hair and a full beard, but looking much younger than his brother, smiled and replied softly:

"About the same as Pierre, maybe four or five."

They always told this lie that made old man Roland happy.

He wrapped the fishing line around the oar lock, crossed his arms, and said:

"I'll definitely never fish in the afternoon again. After ten o'clock, that's it. The fish don't bite anymore, they take a nap in the sun."

The honest man looked around at the water with the satisfaction of an owner.

He was previously a jewelry merchant in Paris, who, because he loved sailing and fishing very much, retired from his accounting work as soon as he had some savings and could live frugally off the interest.

He retired to Le Havre, bought a boat, and became an amateur sailor. His two sons, Pierre and Jean, stayed in Paris to continue their studies, sometimes returning during vacations to share the joys of fishing with their father.

After graduating from middle school, the elder one, Pierre, five years older than Jean, felt he had a talent for various professions in succession, tried half a dozen one after another, quickly became bored with each, and immediately threw himself into new hopes.

Finally, medicine piqued his interest, so he studied it with extraordinary enthusiasm, and as a result, after a fairly short period of study, and after obtaining a special dispensation from the Minister of Education to shorten his studies, he recently passed the doctor's examination. He was an ardent, intelligent, volatile, and obstinate man, full of fantasies and philosophical ideas.

Jean was exactly the opposite of his brother: his brother had black hair, he had golden hair; his brother was irritable, he was calm; his brother was resentful, he was very docile. He studied law steadily, and recently, his brother obtained his doctor's diploma, and he also obtained his bachelor of laws degree at the same time.

So, both brothers came home to rest, and, if things went well, both wanted to practice in Le Havre.

But a vague jealousy, a kind of latent jealousy that grew day by day between brothers and sisters, and that was rarely seen until adulthood, only to erupt when one of them married or received an unexpected blessing, made the two wary of each other in a fraternal, harmless intimacy. True, they were both affectionate, but they coveted each other. Pierre, five years older than Jean, looked at the other little creature suddenly appearing in his parents' arms, and being loved and caressed by them in such a way, with the hostility of a spoiled pet.
